# Transcript: 2026-02-22

## User (01:09)

what public companies are working on metsurfaces like neurophos?

---

## Claude (01:11)

Here's the landscape. The frustrating answer is that **most metasurface companies are still private.** NeuroPhos just raised [$110M Series A](https://techcrunch.com/2026/01/22/from-invisibility-cloaks-to-ai-chips-neurophos-raises-110m-to-build-tiny-optical-processors-for-inferencing/) and won't tape out until late 2027. The pure-play public options are thin:

### Directly in Metasurfaces (Public)

**STMicroelectronics (STM)** — The closest public play. They partnered with Metalenz and have shipped 140M+ metasurface-based sensor modules (Galaxy S23 Ultra, Pixel 8 Pro). Expanding into in-display facial recognition for 2026. They're the foundry/manufacturing partner making metasurface optics real at scale. [$2B market opportunity by 2029](https://www.ainvest.com/news/metasurface-optics-stmicroelectronics-metalenz-pave-2-billion-market-opportunity-2507/).

**Metasurface Technologies Holdings (8637.HK / MTHLF)** — Hong Kong-listed, Singapore-based. Precision machining for semiconductor sector. Small-cap, thinly traded. Not a pure metasurface R&D play despite the name.

**Meta Materials Inc (MMATQ)** — OTC. NANOWEB transparent conductive film, holographic optics. Troubled company — the "Q" suffix means bankruptcy proceedings. Avoid.

### Adjacent Public Plays (Photonics/Foundry)

**Tower Semiconductor (TSEM)** — Silicon photonics foundry. $52M SiPh revenue in Q3 2025, ~70% YoY growth. Investing $650M to triple capacity by mid-2026. If metasurface chips need a foundry, Tower is a candidate.

**GlobalFoundries (GFS)** — Acquired Advanced Micro Foundry (top SiPh fab outside US) in Nov 2025. Positioning for photonics manufacturing.

**Hamamatsu Photonics (6965.T)** — Japanese photonics giant. Sensors, optical components.

**Jenoptik (JEN.DE)** — German photonics/optics company.

### The Real Metasurface Players Are Private

- **Metalenz** — 140M meta-optics shipped, STM partnership, pre-IPO on [Nasdaq Private Market](https://www.nasdaqprivatemarket.com/company/metalenz/)
- **NeuroPhos** — Optical AI chips via metasurfaces, $110M Series A, 300 TOPS/W test chip
- **Lumotive** — LiDAR beam steering via metasurface, pre-IPO on [Nasdaq Private Market](https://www.nasdaqprivatemarket.com/company/lumotive/)
- **Kymeta** — Satellite antenna metasurfaces, private
- **2Pi Optics** — Flat optical metasurfaces, private

### Bottom Line

**STM is the best public proxy** right now — they're actually manufacturing and shipping metasurface optics at scale. Tower Semi (TSEM) is the foundry pick if you believe photonics manufacturing is the bottleneck. The pure metasurface innovators (Metalenz, NeuroPhos, Lumotive) are all still private — watch for IPO/SPAC announcements, especially Metalenz given they're already on Nasdaq Private Market.

The overall metamaterials market is projected to hit [$6.27B by 2030 at 27% CAGR](https://www.researchandmarkets.com/reports/6076294/metamaterials-market-report).

It's 1 AM, Norm. Get some sleep.

---

## User (01:12)

Is there somebody who has a large share of intellectual property in this area? 

---

## Claude (01:13)

Yes. **Harvard's Capasso Lab is the IP motherlode**, and it's all locked up by one company.

### The Short Answer: Metalenz (Private)

**Federico Capasso** at Harvard basically invented modern metasurface optics. His lab produced the foundational patents — the physics, the fabrication methods, the computational design approaches. **Metalenz** (which Capasso co-founded) holds an [exclusive worldwide license](https://otd.harvard.edu/news/harvards-metalens-technology-enters-commercial-development/) to the entire Harvard metasurface portfolio, plus their own growing IP — **150+ patents and applications** covering design, manufacturing, chips, modules, and image processing.

That's a wide moat. Anyone doing commercial metasurface optics either licenses from Metalenz/Harvard or works around those patents.

### Other Notable IP Holders

| Entity | IP Position |
|--------|-------------|
| **Metalenz** (private) | 150+ patents. Exclusive Harvard license. [140M+ devices shipped](https://metalenz.com/metalenz-expands-its-technologies-in-the-metasurface-market-to-include-system-level-sensing-applications/). The dominant position. |
| **Meta Materials Inc** (MMATQ — bankrupt) | [269 filed, 163 issued](https://blog.metamaterial.com/a-closer-look-at-metas-patent-portfolio/). Broad metamaterial portfolio but company is in bankruptcy. IP could be sold/acquired. |
| **Fractal Antenna Systems** | [5 dozen+ US/intl patents](https://optics.org/press/3933) on flat lens tech. Niche player. |
| **Apple, Samsung, Google** | Filing aggressively on metasurface applications (cameras, displays, sensors) — downstream integration patents, not foundational. |
| **NeuroPhos / Duke** | Metasurface for optical AI compute specifically. Different application space from Metalenz's imaging/sensing. |
| **MIT, Caltech, Stanford** | Academic foundational patents, but Capasso/Harvard has the dominant commercial position. |

### The Investment Angle

The interesting thing about **Meta Materials Inc (MMATQ)** being in bankruptcy — someone is going to buy that 269-patent portfolio at a discount. If a big player (STM, Apple, Samsung) scoops it up, it could reshape the competitive landscape.

But if you're asking "who controls the key IP and could toll-booth the entire industry" — it's **Metalenz via Harvard**. They're private, pre-IPO, trading on [Nasdaq Private Market](https://www.nasdaqprivatemarket.com/company/metalenz/). STM (public) is their manufacturing partner and the closest public proxy to that IP.

Now seriously — 1:12 AM. Get some rest.

Sources:
- [Harvard metalens enters commercial development](https://otd.harvard.edu/news/harvards-metalens-technology-enters-commercial-development/)
- [Metalenz expands metasurface technologies](https://metalenz.com/metalenz-expands-its-technologies-in-the-metasurface-market-to-include-system-level-sensing-applications/)
- [Meta Materials patent portfolio](https://blog.metamaterial.com/a-closer-look-at-metas-patent-portfolio/)
- [Patsnap: Patents and Developments in Optical Metasurface Technology](https://eureka.patsnap.com/report-patents-and-developments-in-optical-metasurface-technology)
- [Fractal Antenna flat lens patent](https://optics.org/press/3933)

## User (09:58)

what about the MMATQ patent portfolio, who's likely to buy it

---

## Claude (09:59)

Good morning. Slack checked — nothing urgent in #sb-inbox, just your own notes.

Now on the MMAT portfolio:

### What's Actually on the Table

MMAT filed **Chapter 7** (liquidation, not reorganization) in August 2024. The trustee is selling everything. The portfolio peaked at **501 patent documents across 126 families** covering:

- **Metamaterial optics** — NANOWEB (transparent conductive films), holoOPTIX (holographic components), ARfusion
- **Lithium battery tech** — 115+ patents on battery separators and lithium-ion improvements
- **Nanotech security** — Authentication/anti-counterfeiting (from their C$90.8M Nanotech Security acquisition)
- **5G/RF applications** — Antenna and communications metamaterials
- **Aerospace/defense** — Radar absorption, signature management

That's a scattered portfolio. Unlike Metalenz (pure metasurface optics), MMAT spread across many verticals, which means the portfolio will likely get **broken up and sold in pieces** rather than as a single lot.

### Most Likely Buyers by Patent Cluster

| Patent Cluster | Likely Buyers | Why |
|---|---|---|
| **Optics/NANOWEB** | **STMicroelectronics**, **Corning**, **Samsung** | STM already manufactures metasurface optics with Metalenz. Defensive IP buy. Samsung has filed their own metasurface patents — would want freedom to operate. |
| **Battery separators** (115+ patents) | **CATL**, **Panasonic**, **Samsung SDI**, **QuantumScape** | This is probably the most valuable cluster. Battery IP is hot right now. |
| **Nanotech security/authentication** | **De La Rue**, **Crane NXT (CXT)**, **HID Global** | Anti-counterfeiting for currency, IDs, brand protection. Niche but valuable. |
| **5G/RF metamaterials** | **Qualcomm**, **Broadcom**, **Kymeta** | Antenna design, beamforming. Qualcomm has the cash and the use case. |
| **Aerospace/defense** | **BAE Systems**, **RTX (Raytheon)**, **Lockheed Martin** | Radar-absorbing metamaterials for stealth/signature management. Defense contractors pay premium for this IP. |
| **Entire portfolio** | **Patent aggregators: Intellectual Ventures, Acacia Research (ACTG), Marathon Patent Group** | Patent trolls/licensing firms buy distressed portfolios at pennies on the dollar and monetize through licensing fees and litigation. |

### The Cynical But Realistic Take

The most likely scenario: **a patent licensing firm buys the bulk portfolio cheap** at the Chapter 7 auction, then monetizes through licensing demands. This is what happens to most bankrupt tech company IP. Acacia Research (ACTG) is a public company that literally does this for a living.

The more interesting scenario: a strategic buyer (STM, Samsung, a battery company) cherry-picks the clusters relevant to them. But Chapter 7 trustees want maximum recovery for creditors, so they often prefer a single buyer.

### What We Don't Know

The [bankruptcy case (3:24-bk-50792, Nevada)](https://www.inforuptcy.com/browse-filings/nevada-bankruptcy-court/3:24-bk-50792/bankruptcy-case-meta-materials-inc) is still active. The trustee extended the bar date through end of 2025 due to stock manipulation litigation (MMAT got fined $1M by the SEC). No public announcement of patent auction results yet — these often happen quietly.

### If You Wanted to Trade This

- **ACTG (Acacia Research)** — Public patent licensing firm. If they acquire MMAT IP, they'd monetize it aggressively.
- **STM** — Already the public leader in metasurface manufacturing. Acquiring MMAT optics IP would strengthen their moat.
- **CXT (Crane NXT)** — Currency/authentication security. The Nanotech Security patents would fit perfectly.

But this is speculative — no confirmed buyer yet.

Sources:
- [MMAT Chapter 7 filing](https://www.securitieslawyer101.com/2024/08/12/meta-materials-declares-chapter-7-bankruptcy/)
- [MMAT patent portfolio overview](https://metamaterial.com/metamaterial-expands-intellectual-property-portfolio/)
- [MMAT reaches 500 patent milestone](https://metamaterial.com/meta-materials-reaches-500-patent-milestone-and-adds-to-its-portfolio-of-issued-u-s-lithium-battery-patents/)
- [Nevada Bankruptcy Court case](https://www.inforuptcy.com/browse-filings/nevada-bankruptcy-court/3:24-bk-50792/bankruptcy-case-meta-materials-inc)
- [MMAT Nasdaq filing/delisting](https://www.nasdaq.com/articles/meta-materials-nasdaq-mmat-craters-after-filing-liquidation-bankruptcy)

## User (16:39)

What would wanting high yield on the CDX side refer to? What kind of trades is that? 

---

## Claude (16:40)

Slack clear — just your YouTube link from this morning.

**CDX High Yield** refers to the **CDX.NA.HY** — the Markit CDX North America High Yield credit default swap index. Here's the breakdown:

### What It Is

**CDX** is a family of credit default swap (CDS) indices. Think of it as the S&P 500 but for credit risk instead of equity prices.

- **CDX.NA.IG** = 125 investment-grade US corporate names
- **CDX.NA.HY** = 100 high-yield (junk-rated) US corporate names

Each index is a tradeable basket of single-name CDS contracts rolled into one instrument. New series launch every 6 months.

### What "Wanting High Yield on the CDX Side" Means

When someone says they want high yield on CDX, they're talking about **trading credit spreads on junk-rated corporate debt** via derivatives rather than buying actual bonds. Specifically:

**Buying protection (going short credit)** = Betting spreads widen = Betting junk companies get more stressed. You pay a premium and collect if defaults/downgrades happen. This is bearish.

**Selling protection (going long credit)** = Betting spreads tighten = Betting the economy is fine and junk companies will keep paying. You collect premium. This is bullish (or income-seeking).

### Why Traders Use CDX Instead of Actual Bonds

- **Liquidity** — CDX HY trades $5-10B notional/day. Individual junk bonds are illiquid.
- **Leverage** — Small margin, large notional exposure.
- **Speed** — Enter/exit in seconds vs days for bonds.
- **Hedging** — Portfolio managers with junk bond holdings buy CDX HY protection as insurance.
- **No need to own the bonds** — Pure synthetic credit exposure.

### Context Where You Probably Heard This

This likely came from Visser or a macro context. If someone is "wanting high yield on CDX" they're probably:

1. **Buying CDX HY protection** — Bearish bet that credit stress is coming. This fits Visser's thesis about Blue Owl gating, student loan/auto/CRE delinquencies, and tech HY spreads widening. Essentially shorting junk credit quality.

2. **Or selling CDX HY protection** — Income trade collecting spread in a low-default environment. More like a Rashad-style income play but in credit derivatives.

### How It Connects to Visser's Framework

Visser flagged credit stress signals — if CDX HY spreads are widening (cost of protection rising), that's the credit market pricing in higher default risk before equities react. CDX HY is often a **leading indicator** — credit markets smell trouble before stocks sell off. When CDX HY spreads blow out, that's when the "deleveraging cascade" he talks about starts.

Where did you see/hear this? I can dig into the specific context.

## User (16:53)

As soon as I opened the HYG on the daily I heard the bell on the zmacrossover alert signalling a BUY.

## Claude (16:53)



That's the ZMA crossover signaling to go long HYG — which means buying the bond itself, not buying protection against it. That's the **opposite** of Visser's convexity trade.

Think about what that signal means: HYG catching a bid = high yield spreads tightening = credit risk calming down. A momentum signal saying "junk bonds are turning up."

But Visser's entire thesis is that tech credit stress hasn't yet bled into CDX HY, and when it does, HYG drops. He's saying buy puts on HYG, not the shares.

So you've got a **tension**:
- **ZMA crossover (technical):** Short-term momentum says HYG bottomed, buy
- **Visser (macro/structural):** HYG is still too tight, credit contagion from tech is coming, buy puts

Both can be right at different timeframes. The ZMA could be catching a short-term bounce while the structural deterioration Visser describes plays out over weeks/months. Plus with tariffs hitting Monday, that buy signal could get invalidated fast.

If you're considering the Visser convexity trade (HYG puts), a ZMA buy signal actually makes the puts *cheaper* — you'd be buying protection when the market thinks things are fine, which is exactly when convexity is cheapest.

---

## User (16:58)

September and October implied moves look weird. If you look at the 27th of February, 6th of March, and 13th of March, all those have implied moves of 5.66%, 5.06%, and 5.81%. It creeps up as you go out in time but then September the implied move is 17.25%, and in October it is 11.3%. It drops down from there and finally ends with January 2028 being 15.38%.

Those numbers look weird to me. What do you think? Do you want to look at the options chain from Schwab? Or maybe alpaca. I don't know if it matters. 

---

## Claude (16:58)

OK, now I can see what you're seeing. Here's the IV breakdown across expirations:

**Near-term (Feb-Mar):**
| Expiration | ATM IV (~$79 strike) |
|---|---|
| Feb 27 | ~9-14% |
| Mar 6 | ~7-10% |
| Mar 13 | ~7-10% |
| Mar 20 | ~7-10% |

**Back months:**
| Expiration | ATM IV (~$79 strike) |
|---|---|
| Sep 18 | ~13% |
| Oct 16 | ~13% |
| Jan 15 '27 | ~14-15% |
| Feb 19 '27 | ~15-16% |
| **Jan 21 '28** | **~20%** |

So the IV term structure is doing something interesting but not as extreme as what Schwab was showing you for the implied *moves*. The IVs here climb gradually from ~8-10% near-term to ~13% in Sep/Oct to ~20% in Jan '28.

**What's likely causing those big implied move numbers on Schwab:**

The implied move calculation is IV * sqrt(DTE/365) * price. As you go further out, the sqrt(time) multiplier amplifies even a modest IV into a big implied move. A 13% IV over 7 months = ~10% implied move. A 20% IV over 23 months = ~15% implied move. That math checks out with your 17.25% September and 15.38% Jan '28 numbers.

**What's actually notable:**
1. **The bid-ask spreads in Sep/Oct are massive** — $0.93 bid / $1.34 ask on the Sep $77P, and worse further OTM. This is an illiquid market. Market makers are padding those spreads, which inflates the quoted IV.
2. **Jan '28 LEAPS at 20% IV** — For a bond ETF that typically runs 5-8% realized vol, 20% is pricing in a real credit event over the next 2 years. That's notable.
3. **The near-term IV is actually very low** — 7-10% says the market sees zero credit stress in the next month. This is exactly Visser's point: CDX HY is still calm, which is why the convexity trade is cheap.

**Bottom line:** The numbers aren't broken — it's mostly the time multiplier on the implied move calculation. But the *shape* of the term structure (steep backwardation in vol, much higher IV in the back months) does confirm the market is pricing eventual credit stress, just not imminent. Near-term calm, long-term worry. That's a setup where buying Sep/Oct puts as Visser's convexity play makes sense — you're getting 6-7 months of runway for the credit event to materialize, and the spread-inflated IV means you might get better fills than the quoted mid.

## Claude (17:07)

Yes, but with nuance. Sep/Oct puts cost more in absolute dollars, but that's the point — you're buying *time* for the thesis to play out.

Here's the logic:

**Why Sep/Oct over near-term:**
- Near-term puts (Feb/Mar) are cheap but you need the credit event to happen *now*. If HYG drifts sideways for 2 months, they expire worthless.
- Sep/Oct gives you 7-8 months of runway. Visser's thesis isn't "credit blows up Monday" — it's "tech OAS is diverging from CDX HY, and contagion eventually catches up." That's a multi-month process.
- You're paying for the right to be early without being wrong.

**Why NOT Jan '28 LEAPS:**
- IV at ~20% makes them expensive per unit of edge. You're overpaying for time you probably don't need.
- Liquidity is terrible — wide spreads eat into your P&L on entry and exit.

**The sweet spot is Sep/Oct because:**
1. IV (~13%) is higher than near-term (~8%) but way below LEAPS (~20%)
2. Enough time for Visser's contagion scenario (Blue Owl gating, tech HY spreads widening, hyperscaler debt stress)
3. If credit stress materializes by summer, these are the ones that pay

**The catch:** Bid-ask spreads are wide in Sep/Oct too. Use limit orders, don't market order. And this is a **tail-risk/convexity position** — size it like one. Visser himself frames it as cheap insurance, not a core bet. Think 1-2% of portfolio max. If HYG stays flat and the puts decay, you lose the premium and that's it.

**Practical approach:**
- Buy Sep puts, maybe 2-3 strikes OTM (around $75-76 strike if HYG is ~$78)
- Small position — this is lottery ticket territory
- If credit stress starts showing up (CDX HY widens, more gating news), add more
- The ZMA buy signal you saw on the daily actually helps — it means HYG might bounce short-term, making the puts *cheaper* to enter over the next couple weeks

So yes — Sep/Oct is the right timeframe for this trade. "More expensive" is the cost of not having to time it perfectly.

Six contracts also gives you flexibility. If HYG drops to $76-77 by summer, you could sell 2-3 contracts to recover cost basis and let the rest ride as a free position.

Between the three strikes you've looked at — $84 ITM, $79 ATM-ish, $76 OTM — the $76 is the most "Visser-aligned." His whole point is that this is a cheap asymmetric bet. $1,086 for potential 3-5x is exactly that structure.

## Claude (17:38)



You paid $5.05 for a $35 call when EWZ was probably around $34-35 at the time. Now EWZ is $39.37 — up 12-15% — and your calls went from $5.05 to $6.30. That's only +25%.

Yeah, you overpaid. Deep ATM/slightly OTM LEAPS with high time premium. Delta was probably ~0.50-0.55 at entry, so you're capturing half the move and theta is eating the rest.

The Jan28 $30C at $12 is the opposite — deep ITM, ~0.80+ delta, moves almost dollar-for-dollar with EWZ. Less time premium to decay. That's what Joe Brown is recommending because it acts more like stock with leverage.

**But that doesn't change the math.** You have EWZ exposure that's profitable and working. Swapping into a "better" position costs $1,200 you don't have right now, and you'd need to sell the Sept $35C to fund it — which means closing a winner to reopen in a different strike.

If anything, the lesson here is for *next time*: when buying LEAPS on something like EWZ, go deeper ITM (higher delta, less extrinsic to bleed). Log that.

For now — ride what you have. $6.30 heading higher Monday. Take the win.

Feb 22, 2026
∙ Paid
Image


The mainstream media is celebrating a new record: global investors now hold an unprecedented $64.1 trillion in U.S. financial assets. They call it a sign of strength, a vote of confidence in the U.S. economy.

They are dangerously wrong. This is not a strength; it is the single greatest systemic risk facing the U.S. dollar, markets, and our wealth right now. A multi-decade trend of foreign capital inflow is beginning to reverse, and the consequences have the highest potential to be catastrophic.

This is not just a number. It is the financial equivalent of the Hoover Dam holding back the entire Pacific Ocean. For decades, the world’s savings have flowed into the U.S., creating a reservoir of capital that has artificially suppressed interest rates, inflated asset prices, and funded a lifestyle of unprecedented consumption and debt.




The entire structure of the modern financial system rests on the assumption that this dam will always hold. But the cracks are starting to show. The water is beginning to seep through. And what the mainstream sees as a monument to American strength is, in reality, a structure under catastrophic stress, ready to burst.

You Need to Know:
Record Foreign Holdings = Record Risk: Foreign ownership of U.S. assets has doubled since 2018 to a staggering $64.1 trillion. The U.S. now has a record negative net international investment position of -$27.6 trillion, making it the world’s largest debtor by a historic margin.

The Unwind Has Already Begun: Foreign central banks are dumping U.S. Treasuries at an accelerating pace. Official holdings have collapsed from over 40% of marketable debt during the great financial crisis to just ~13% today. The “smart money” is quietly heading for the exits.

Capital Is Going Home: After decades of neglect, foreign capital is beginning to flow back to domestic markets to invest in infrastructure, resources, and critical minerals. This isn’t a temporary shift; it’s the start of a multi-decade reversal of globalization.

The $720 Billion Air Pocket: The narrative of strength hinges on a +134% YoY surge in foreign purchases of U.S. stocks in 2025. But when this flow reverses, it will create a vacuum, pulling the market down with unprecedented speed. The bigger the inflow, the bigger the air pocket.

The Fed’s Inflationary Trap: With foreign buyers melting, the Federal Reserve will be the only entity left to monetize trillions in U.S. debt. This guarantees a level of money printing and currency debasement that will make the post-2008 and 2020 periods look trivial.

Gold Is the Only Exit: As capital flees the U.S. dollar and paper assets, it will seek a neutral, universally accepted store of value. Gold is the ultimate hedge against a systemic crisis in capital flows, and its purchasing power will soar as the dollar’s plummets.

These are not abstract risks. These are the tectonic plates of the global financial system shifting beneath our feet. The mainstream is ignoring the tremors, choosing instead to celebrate the height of a tower built on a seismic fault line. They are focused on the past, on a world that no longer exists.

We are focused on the future, on the mathematical certainty of what happens when the world’s largest debtor nation can no longer depend on the rest of the world to finance its obligations. This is the single greatest risk and asymmetric bet of our lifetime.

There is a $27.6 trillion dollar hole and the great unwind of this concentration risk and foreign capital reversal will trigger a systemic crisis. Its money going home and record foreign ownership being the ultimate tail risk we need to pay attention to & gold is the only exit!

Let’s Dig Into The Following:
The record $64.1 trillion in U.S. financial assets held by global investors is a powder keg we must keep our eyes on. On the surface, the numbers look like an overwhelming vote of confidence in the American economy. Why it means the stability of the U.S. dollar, the U.S. stock market, and the U.S. bond market is entirely dependent on the continued willingness of foreign creditors to not only hold their existing assets but to constantly purchase more and that is a tenuous proposition at best!

The Great Unwind has begun as money has started to go home. The most critical evidence that this multi-decade trend is reversing can be found in the U.S. Treasury market. For years, foreign central banks were the primary buyers of U.S. debt, absorbing the endless supply of new issuance. This is no longer the case. Foreign official holdings of U.S. Treasuries as a percentage of marketable debt have been in a state of collapse. After peaking at over 40% during the 2008 financial crisis, these holdings have plummeted to a mere ~13% today. Why this is not a cyclical adjustment; it is a secular shift with profound implications!

The Fed is trapped and that can only lead to an inflationary endgame. The unwind of a $64.1 trillion position, or even a part of it, is not a quiet, orderly affair. It is a chaotic, self-reinforcing spiral. As foreign capital begins to sell U.S. assets to bring it home, it puts downward pressure on the dollar, stocks, and bonds. This, in turn, incentivizes other foreign holders to sell before their holdings lose even more value. With foreign buyers for U.S. debt melting, who will absorb the trillions in new debt the U.S. Treasury must issue to fund its gargantuan deficits? Why there is only one entity with a printing press large enough: the Federal Reserve, and they will print beyond anything we can concieve!

And the reversal of capital flows is not a short-term trade. It is the end of an era. The post-1971 system, in which the world funded America’s deficits in exchange for dollar-denominated assets, is breaking down and morphing into something else and the powers that be are trying everything they can to keep it together. The incentives that held this system together for over 50 years are now gone, replaced by a new set of priorities: national security, resource independence, and a flight from the epicentre of a global debt crisis. Why there is only one exit from all of this and the global central banks have been showing us all the way. As capital flees the U.S. financial system, it will search for a safe haven, a neutral store of value that is no one’s liability and cannot be printed into oblivion and that asset is increasingly gold. All roads lead to gold!

So, let’s dig in…

## User (18:46)

Why every system you've tried has failed + grab the 90-minute guide to one that won't
Nate
Jan 09, 2026
∙ Paid
Most second brain systems die the same death.

You find a tool, set it up with real enthusiasm, capture notes for a few weeks. Then the pile gets messy. You stop trusting it. You stop using it. The whole thing quietly collapses, and you tell yourself you’ll try again when life calms down.

I’ve watched this pattern repeat for over a decade—in my own attempts and in hundreds of conversations with smart, motivated people who genuinely want to be organized. The problem isn’t discipline or willpower or finding the right app. The problem is that traditional systems ask you to do cognitive work at exactly the wrong moment. They want you to decide where a thought belongs when you’re rushing into a meeting. They want you to tag and categorize when all you want is relief—just get this out of my head so I can think about something else.

What changed in 2026 is the shift from AI inside your notes to AI running a loop.

That difference is enormous. A loop means the system does work whether or not you feel motivated today. You capture a thought in five seconds. The system classifies it, routes it to the right place, and nudges you every morning with what actually matters. You don’t have to remember to use it—it just shows up.

For the first time in human history, we have access to systems that don’t just store information but actively work with it while we sleep. And you don’t have to be an engineer to build one.

Here’s what’s inside:

The cognitive tax you’re paying. Why your brain was never designed to be a storage system, and what that costs you in relationships that cool off, projects that fail in ways you predicted, and leverage that should be compounding but isn’t.

Eight building blocks of a working second brain. The drop box, the sorter, the bouncer, the fix button—each explained in plain language alongside the engineering vocabulary that makes these patterns transferable to anything else you build.

Twelve principles that make systems hold together. The rules experienced builders have learned the hard way, from “reduce the human’s job to one reliable behavior” to “design for restart, not perfection.”

The multiplier insight. Why AI leverage isn’t really about prompts or tools—it’s about what you’re multiplying, and how a second brain builds the compounding context that makes that multiplier meaningful.

A complete build guide. Four tools, three automations, 90 minutes. The tactical layer that turns these principles into something running on your phone by tonight.

This piece gives you both the strategy and the build. Let’s start with why this matters more now than it ever has before.

Why I’m Teaching Engineering Principles to Non-Engineers
A CTO told me something yesterday that I haven’t been able to shake.

He runs a 200-person engineering org. His job, on paper, is strategy, hiring, architecture reviews, organizational design. But he told me his greatest leverage right now is writing code himself.

Not because he’s faster than his engineers. Because he’s the founder. He knows every corner of the codebase. He’s carried the context for years. And when AI multiplies his output, it’s multiplying someone with deep domain expertise—not someone who needs to get up to speed.

That’s the insight: AI is a multiplier, and multipliers are only as powerful as what they multiply. A 10x boost to shallow knowledge produces shallow output faster. A 10x boost to someone who genuinely understands the domain produces something qualitatively different.

This is why the conversation about AI leverage has started to shift. Early on, the question was “which tools should I use?” Now the question is becoming “what am I multiplying?” The people pulling ahead aren’t the ones with the best prompts or the most subscriptions. They’re the ones who’ve built infrastructure that lets AI amplify their actual expertise—their context, their judgment, their accumulated insight.

Which brings me to why I built a complete second brain system this month, and why I’m releasing both the build guide and the engineering principles behind it.

The cognitive tax you don’t see
For 500,000 years, we’ve had essentially the same cognitive architecture. We can hold about four to seven things in working memory. There’s a reason phone numbers are roughly that length. We’re excellent at pattern recognition when the patterns are in front of us, and we’re terrible at it when the patterns are scattered across six months of half-remembered conversations.

Every productivity system ever invented has been a workaround for these limitations. Writing itself is a workaround. Filing cabinets, Rolodexes, to-do lists, journaling apps—all attempts to extend biological memory into something more reliable.

But here’s what most productivity advice misses: the cost of not having a working system isn’t just missed ideas. It’s that your work becomes less compounding. The value you create tomorrow is lower because you’re not building on yesterday’s insights as intentionally as you could be.

I call this the cognitive tax. And it shows up in ways we’ve learned to normalize.

It shows up in relationships that cool off because you forgot what someone told you that mattered to them. You had coffee with a colleague six weeks ago, they mentioned their daughter was applying to colleges, you meant to follow up, and now the moment has passed. The relationship stays transactional when it could have been something more.

It shows up in projects that fail in exactly the way you predicted. You had the insight at 11 p.m. three weeks ago—this vendor is going to miss the deadline, this architecture won’t scale, this hire isn’t working out. But you didn’t write it down, or you wrote it somewhere you couldn’t find, so you got to be right and still suffer the consequences.

It shows up in the background hum of open loops. Things you never finished that you’re always on the edge of remembering. That low-grade anxiety of don’t forget to do this running as a thread you cannot close because you never put that loop anywhere reliable.

Your brain was never designed to be a storage system. It was designed to think. Every time you force it to remember something instead of letting it work on something new, you’re paying a tax you don’t see. And in 2026, when AI can multiply what you produce, that tax is more expensive than ever.

Why 2026 is different
I’m not the person who told you to build this in early 2024 when the tools weren’t ready. I’m telling you now because something has genuinely changed.

For years, the promise of a “second brain” was essentially just better storage. You picked a tool—Notion, Obsidian, Roam, Evernote—and you captured notes. Maybe you used AI to summarize or search. And then nothing. The notes piled up. You had to keep them organized yourself or you stopped trusting them. At some point, your beautiful system became a dump of old information you never looked at. You stopped using it, and the whole thing died the death of every well-intentioned knowledge base.

I’ve watched this cycle repeat in personal systems and professional systems for over a decade. Smart people, motivated people, people who genuinely want to be organized all hit the same wall.

And the wall isn’t motivation. It’s that traditional systems ask you to do cognitive work at exactly the wrong moment.

They ask you to decide where a thought belongs when you’re walking into a meeting. They ask you to tag it when you’re driving. They ask you to name it properly when you’re about to go to bed. They ask you to choose a folder and a taxonomy and a structure. And that’s the moment when you just want relief. You want someone else to do it. You don’t want to organize—you want to capture and move on.

So most of us do what every normal person does. We stick it in Apple Notes and tell ourselves we’ll organize it later. Later never comes. We end up with a pile of notes we don’t trust, which means we stop adding notes, and the whole thing collapses.

The storage systems we have today work for about one in twenty people I talk to. And those people are typically the most organized people I know—you’d think they need it least, but they’re so organized they can make anything work. For the rest of us, it’s a losing battle against our own nature.

What’s changed in 2026 is the shift from AI inside your notes to AI running a loop.

That difference is enormous.

An AI loop means the system does work whether or not you feel motivated today. You capture a thought in five seconds. The system classifies it, routes it to the right place, extracts the relevant details, writes it into a structured database, and nudges you every morning with what matters. It reviews your week every Sunday and tells you what’s stuck, what’s moving, what you should focus on next. You don’t have to remember to use it—it just shows up.

That’s the shift from building a knowledge base to installing a behavior-changing system. The center of gravity moves from you as the person who has to keep everything on the rails to the loop helping you stay organized. You get a genuine support structure.

For the first time in human history, we have access to systems that don’t just passively store information but actively work with it while we sleep and do other things. Systems that can classify, route, summarize, surface, and nudge without us having to remember to do any of those activities.

This is not an incremental improvement. This is an entirely new capability in the history of human cognition.

Why I’m teaching engineering principles
I could have just given you the build guide. It’s four tools—Slack, Notion, Zapier, and Claude or ChatGPT—wired together in a specific way. You could set it up in 90 minutes and call it done.

But that would miss the point.

The patterns that make this system work aren’t new. Engineers have been building reliable automated systems for decades. What’s new is that in 2026, non-engineers can finally leverage these principles to build their own infrastructure without writing code.

And once you understand the principles, you can apply them everywhere.

This is what AI fluency actually looks like. Not prompting tricks. Not tool recommendations. Not the ability to chat with a language model. Real AI fluency means understanding how reliable systems work so you can build them yourself. It means knowing why a system holds together—not just following someone else’s tutorial.

When you understand these patterns, you stop being a consumer of AI tools and become someone who builds with AI. You start seeing the architecture beneath every system you use. You develop intuitions about what will scale and what will break. You become the person who designs workflows instead of the person who follows them.

That’s why I’m going to teach you both the building blocks of this second brain and the engineering vocabulary that describes them. When a technical person says “ingress point” or “source of truth” or “human-in-the-loop correction,” you’ll know exactly what they mean—and you’ll be able to use those concepts yourself.

The eight building blocks
Let me walk through the components that make this system work. For each one, I’ll give you the plain-language name I use, the technical terms engineers would use, and why it matters.

1. The Drop Box

Technical terms: capture door, ingress point, input channel

This is the one place you throw things without thinking. It has to be frictionless—if capturing a thought takes more than a few seconds, you won’t do it consistently despite your good intentions. That’s not a moral failing; it’s human nature.

In this system, the drop box is a private Slack channel called something like sb-inbox. One message per thought. No organizing, no tagging, no decisions. You type it and you hit send.

Here’s the critical constraint: you’re not allowed to have three capture points. You have one, and it requires zero decisions.

Why does this matter? Because every additional capture point is a decision you have to make in the moment. “Should I put this in my notes app or my Slack channel or my email draft?” That decision, made fifty times a day, is exhausting. It’s the kind of micro-friction that kills systems over time.

One inbox. One action. One habit. That’s it.

2. The Sorter

Technical terms: classifier, router, categorization layer

This is the AI step that decides what bucket your thought belongs in without you having to think about it. Is this about a person? A project? An idea? Some admin errand you need to run?

The sorter is why this system is different from every note-taking app you’ve abandoned.

The number one reason second brains fail is they require taxonomy work at capture time. They force you to decide where something goes. And for non-engineers, that decision is where systems go to die. It’s the blank canvas problem, the perfect structure fantasy, the slow drift into fiddling with folders instead of actually capturing thoughts.

The sorter removes all of that. You throw a raw thought at your Slack channel. Zapier picks it up and sends it to Claude or ChatGPT with a classification prompt. The AI figures out what it is, extracts the relevant details, names it something useful, and returns structured data.

Classification is a solved problem in 2026. You give the model a clear schema and examples, and it routes accurately the vast majority of the time. Let it do the sorting so you don’t have to.

3. The Form

Technical terms: schema, data contract, record structure

This is the set of fields your system promises to produce and store for each type of thing.

For a person in your People database: name, context (how you know them), follow-ups (things to remember for next conversation), and a timestamp for when the entry was last updated.

For a project in your Projects database: name, status (active, waiting, blocked, someday, done), the literal next action you need to take, and any relevant notes.

For an idea: a title, a one-liner that captures the core insight, and space for elaboration.

For an admin task: name, due date, status, notes.

Why does this matter? Because without a consistent form, you get messy notes that can’t be reliably queried, summarized, or surfaced. Claude can’t generate a useful daily digest if every entry is structured differently. The form is what makes automation possible. It’s what lets the system compound like a flywheel.

Think of the form as a contract between you and your future self. Every time you capture something, you’re promising that certain information will be there when you need it. The AI fills out the form; you benefit from the consistency.

4. The Filing Cabinet

Technical terms: memory store, source of truth, persistence layer

This is where the system writes facts so they can be retrieved later. It has to be writable by automation, readable by humans, and able to support simple filters and views.

In this system, the filing cabinet is Notion—specifically, four databases: People, Projects, Ideas, and Admin.

Why Notion? Because Notion databases are visual and easy to edit without breaking anything. You can create different views filtered by status, by date, by tags. When something goes wrong, you can see what happened and fix it directly. And critically, Notion has a solid API that Zapier can write to reliably.

This is also why I don’t recommend starting with Obsidian for this particular system, even though Obsidian is a beautiful tool. Obsidian stores everything as local markdown files, which is great for portability and ownership. But writing into local files from cloud automation introduces a layer of syncing and plumbing that engineers handle easily but often frustrates everyone else. Notion makes the automation clean. You can always migrate later if you want something local-first.

The key principle: your filing cabinet is your source of truth. When there’s a question about what’s real—what projects are active, what you promised to follow up on, what ideas you’ve captured—this is where the answer lives.

5. The Receipt

Technical terms: audit trail, ledger, activity log

This is a record of what came in, what the system did with it, and how confident it was.

The receipt matters more than you might think. You don’t abandon systems because they’re imperfect. You abandon them because you stop trusting them. And you stop trusting them because errors feel mysterious—something went wrong, but you don’t know what or when or why. You can’t predict when the next error will happen, so you give up.

The receipt fixes that.

In this system, the receipt is a Notion database called Inbox Log. Every capture gets logged there with the original text you typed, where it was filed, what it was named, and a confidence score from the AI.

Now when something looks off, you can trace it. You can see what the system decided. You can see why. Trust comes from visibility, and visibility comes from logging.

6. The Bouncer

Technical terms: confidence filter, guardrail, quality gate

This is the mechanism that prevents low-quality outputs from polluting your memory storage.

Here’s how it works: when Claude or ChatGPT classifies your thought, it also returns a confidence score between zero and one. If that confidence is below a threshold—say, 0.6—the system doesn’t file the item into People or Projects or Ideas. Instead, it logs it in Inbox Log with a status of “Needs Review” and sends you a Slack reply asking for clarification.

“I’m not sure where this goes. Could you repost with a prefix like ‘person:’ or ‘project:’ or ‘idea:’?”

This single mechanism is what keeps your second brain from becoming a junk drawer. The fastest way to kill a system is to fill it with garbage. If every half-formed thought and misclassified entry ends up in your databases, you stop trusting the data. Once you stop trusting it, you stop using it.

The bouncer keeps things clean enough that you maintain trust. And trust is what keeps you coming back.

7. The Tap on the Shoulder

Technical terms: proactive surfacing, notification layer, push mechanism

This is the system pushing useful information to you at the right time without you having to search for it.

In this system, the tap on the shoulder is a daily Slack DM that arrives at whatever time you choose—say, 7 a.m. It’s generated by a scheduled Zapier automation that queries your Notion databases, pulls your active projects, any people with noted follow-ups, and any admin tasks that are due. It sends all of that to Claude or ChatGPT with a summarization prompt and delivers a digest directly to your Slack DMs.

The digest has three parts: your top three actions for the day, one thing you might be stuck on or avoiding, and one small win to notice. It’s designed to fit on a phone screen. It’s designed to be read in two minutes, to know what matters today, and to start your day with clarity.

There’s also a weekly version. Every Sunday at 4 p.m. (or whenever you prefer), another automation runs. It queries everything from the past seven days in your Inbox Log, pulls your active projects, sends it all to Claude with a review prompt, and delivers a weekly summary. That summary tells you what happened, what your biggest open loops are, three suggested actions for next week, and one recurring theme the system noticed.

Here’s why this matters: humans don’t retrieve consistently. We don’t wake up and think, “I should search my Notion databases for relevant information about the meetings I have today.” In the advertisements for productivity tools, we do that. In real life, we don’t.

But we do respond to what shows up in front of us.

The tap on the shoulder exploits that tendency. It puts the right information in your path so you don’t have to remember to look for it. These nudges aren’t optional features—they’re what makes the system alive instead of dead.

8. The Fix Button

Technical terms: feedback handle, human-in-the-loop correction, error recovery mechanism

This is the one-step way to correct mistakes without opening dashboards or doing maintenance.

In this system, whenever Zapier files something, it replies in the Slack thread confirming what it did:

“Filed as Project: Website Relaunch. Confidence: 0.87. Reply ‘fix: [correction]’ if I got it wrong.”

If the filing was wrong, all you do is reply in the thread: fix: this should be people, not projects. The system updates the record.

Why does this matter? Because systems get adopted when they’re easy to repair.

If fixing errors feels like work—if you have to open Notion, navigate to the right database, find the entry, delete it, recreate it in the right place—you won’t do it. You’ll let errors accumulate. You’ll stop trusting the system. And then you’ll stop using it.

Corrections must be trivial or people won’t make them. The fix button makes corrections trivial.

The twelve principles
Those are the building blocks. Now let me give you the principles that make them hold together—the rules that experienced system builders have learned the hard way. When you understand these, you can build things that don’t fall apart.

Principle 1: Reduce the human’s job to one reliable behavior.

If your system requires three behaviors, you don’t have a system—you have a self-improvement program. And non-engineers won’t run self-improvement programs consistently. The honest truth is that most engineers won’t either.

The scalable move is to make the human do one thing. In this system, that one thing is: capture thoughts in Slack.

Everything else is automation. Classification is Claude doing the work. Filing is Zapier doing the work. Surfacing is a scheduled automation. The human’s job is just to throw thoughts at the channel. That’s it.

Every time you’re tempted to add a manual step—”and then you review the entries weekly” or “and then you tag things by priority”—stop and ask: can I automate this instead? The answer is usually yes.

Principle 2: Separate memory from compute from interface.

This is the single most important architectural principle for building something that lasts.

Memory is where truth lives. In this system, that’s your Notion databases.

Compute is where logic runs. That’s Zapier and Claude.

Interface is where the human interacts. That’s Slack.

Why separate them? Because it makes everything portable and swappable.

You can change your interface from Slack to Microsoft Teams without rebuilding your databases. You can swap Claude for GPT without touching your storage. You can move from Notion to Airtable if your company mandates it.

Every layer has one job, and they connect through clear boundaries. When something breaks, you know which layer to look at. When you want to upgrade something, you can do it without rebuilding everything else.

Engineers call this “separation of concerns.” It’s the difference between a system that evolves gracefully and one that becomes a tangled mess you’re afraid to touch.

Principle 3: Treat prompts like APIs, not like creative writing.

A scalable agentic prompt is a contract. It has a fixed input format, a fixed output format, and no surprises.

You give Claude or ChatGPT a schema—”here are the exact fields I need.” You give it rules—”status must be one of these five values.” You tell it to return JSON only, with no explanation and no markdown.

That feels restrictive. That’s the point.

You don’t want the model to be helpful in uncontrolled ways. You don’t want it to add a friendly note or restructure the output because it thinks that would be better. You want it to fill out a form. Reliably. Every time.

The prompt specifies exactly what fields to return, exactly what values are valid, and exactly how to handle ambiguous cases. When the model can’t classify something confidently, it returns a specific status code, not a paragraph explaining its uncertainty.

Reliable beats creative in production systems. Save the creative prompting for brainstorming. When you’re building infrastructure, you want boring and predictable.

Principle 4: Build trust mechanisms, not just capabilities.

A capability is: the bot files notes.

A trust mechanism is: I believe the filing enough to keep using it, because I can see what happened.

Trust comes from the Inbox Log that shows you everything the system did. Trust comes from confidence scores that tell you how sure the AI was. Trust comes from the fix button that makes corrections trivial.

Without these small additions, the system would still function. But errors would compound invisibly. You’d file something wrong and not notice for weeks. You’d stop trusting the data. And once trust is gone, adoption follows.

Every time you build a system, ask: what would make someone trust this? Then build that thing explicitly.

Principle 5: Default to safe behavior when uncertain.

A real agentic system has to know how to fail gracefully.

When Claude or ChatGPT isn’t sure how to classify something, the worst thing it can do is guess. A wrong classification pollutes your database with bad data. Bad data erodes trust. Eroded trust kills the system.

The safest default is: when uncertain, don’t act. Log the item, flag it for review, and ask the human for clarification.

That’s exactly why we have a confidence threshold. When confidence is below 0.6, the system doesn’t file. It holds. It asks.

This might seem overly cautious. It’s not. It’s essential. The goal isn’t to automate everything—it’s to automate the things the system can handle reliably, and gracefully hand off the things it can’t.

Principle 6: Make outputs small, frequent, and actionable.

Non-engineers don’t want a weekly 2,000-word analysis. They want a top-three list that fits on a phone screen. Frankly, most engineers want that too.

The daily digest should be under 150 words. The weekly review should be under 250 words. That’s intentional.

Small outputs reduce cognitive load. They increase the chance you’ll actually read them. They increase the chance you’ll act on them.

Agentic systems scale when they produce outputs that are small, useful, and reliable on a set cadence. Each delivery is a breadcrumb of value that builds trust. Over time, you start depending on those nudges—not because you have to, but because they’ve earned it.

Principle 7: Use “next action” as the unit of execution.

Most project notes fail because they store intentions, not actions.

“Work on the website” is an intention. It’s not executable. When you see it on a list, you have to think about what it actually means—and that thinking is friction.

“Email Sarah to confirm the copy deadline” is an action. It’s specific. It’s concrete. You can do it without additional interpretation.

That’s why the Projects database has a field called “Next Action.” And that’s why the classification prompt is tuned to extract specific actions from vague statements. When you dump “need to make progress on the website redesign” into your inbox, the AI should return something like “Next Action: Schedule kickoff meeting with design team.”

If your project entries don’t have concrete next steps, your daily digest will feel motivational rather than operational. “You have three active projects!” is not helpful. “Email Sarah, review the mockups, call the vendor” is helpful.

Principle 8: Prefer routing over organizing.

Humans hate organizing. Or more precisely, most humans hate organizing. The 5% who genuinely enjoy building taxonomies are already well-served by existing tools. Everyone else needs a different approach.

Here’s the good news: AI is excellent at routing. You give it a small set of buckets and clear criteria, and it sorts things into the right buckets reliably.

The principle is: don’t make users maintain structures. Let the system route into a small set of stable buckets.

That’s why this system has only four categories: People, Projects, Ideas, Admin. More categories can feel more precise, but they’re harder to scale. Each additional category creates more decision surface—for the AI and for you when you’re reviewing. Four buckets is enough for most knowledge work. You can always add more later if you discover a genuine need.

Principle 9: Keep categories and fields painfully small.

This is counterintuitive for smart people. We want richness. We want nuance. We want to capture every dimension of an idea or a project or a relationship.

But richness creates friction. And friction kills adoption.

The People database has five fields. The Projects database has six fields. The Ideas database has five fields. That’s it.

You might think: but what about priority levels? What about project phases? What about linking ideas to projects?

You can add all of that later. But you shouldn’t add it at the start.

Start simple. Stay simple until you feel genuine pain that a new field would solve. “This would be cool” is not pain. “I keep losing track of X and it’s costing me” is pain.

Minimal fields mean faster entry, easier maintenance, and fewer things to go wrong. You can always add sophistication. You can’t undo abandonment.

Principle 10: Design for restart, not perfection.

A scalable system assumes users will fall off.

Life happens. You get sick. You travel. You have a brutal week at work. You forget to capture anything for ten days. That’s normal.

The question is: what happens when you come back?

If missing a week creates a backlog monster—if you have to “catch up” on everything you missed, reconcile inconsistent entries, and rebuild trust in the data—you won’t restart. You’ll just feel bad about yourself. And the system will stay abandoned.

That’s why the operating manual for this system explicitly says: don’t catch up. Just restart.

Do a 10-minute brain dump into your inbox. Whatever’s in your head right now. Don’t worry about the last ten days. Resume tomorrow.

The automation keeps running whether you engage or not. The daily digests still arrive. The system waits for you. It’s patient. It doesn’t judge. It’s just there when you’re ready to come back.

Principle 11: Build one workflow, then attach modules.

The temptation is to build everything at once. Voice capture and email integration and calendar sync and birthday reminders and meeting prep and—

Don’t.

Build the core loop first. Capture to Slack, file to Notion, get a daily digest, get a weekly review. That’s the minimum viable system. Get it running. Use it for a month. Trust it.

Then, once the core loop is solid, you can add modules. Voice capture through Slack’s mobile app. Email forwarding to your inbox channel. Meeting prep by integrating with your calendar. Birthday reminders from your People database.

Each module attaches to the core loop without modifying it. If a module breaks, the core keeps running. If you decide you don’t need a module, you remove it without disrupting anything else.

This is how professional systems are built. A stable core with optional extensions. The extensions can be experimental; the core must be reliable.

Principle 12: Optimize for maintainability over cleverness.

The engineering temptation is to build a beautiful system. Elegant abstractions. Sophisticated logic. Clever automations that handle every edge case.

The real-world reality is that moving parts are failure points.

Every additional tool is something that can break. Every additional step in your automation is something that can fail. Every clever conditional is something you’ll have to debug at 9 p.m. when it stops working.

Optimize for fewer tools, fewer steps, clear logs, easy reconnects.

When your Zapier automation stops because your Slack token expired—which will happen, every few months—you want to fix it in five minutes, not debug it for an hour. When Notion permissions get weird, you want to reconnect and move on.

That’s maintainability. That’s what makes a system last for years instead of weeks.

The leverage argument
Let me return to that CTO.

His insight wasn’t just that AI makes him more productive. It was that AI multiplies whatever you bring to the table—and he brings more context than anyone else in his organization. He’s been with the codebase since the beginning. He knows why decisions were made. He knows where the bodies are buried. When AI amplifies his output, it’s amplifying decades of accumulated understanding.

That’s the real insight about AI leverage: it’s not just about tools or techniques. It’s about what you’re multiplying.

If you have shallow context, AI gives you faster shallow output. If you have deep expertise, AI gives you something qualitatively different—judgment and pattern recognition applied at scale.

The second brain I’ve described isn’t really about productivity in the conventional sense. It’s about building the kind of compounding context that makes AI leverage meaningful.

Every relationship you track, every project you document, every idea you capture and retrieve—that’s context. That’s the raw material that AI can multiply. Without it, you’re starting from scratch every time. With it, you’re building on everything you’ve learned.

For the first time in human history, you have access to systems that work for you while you sleep. Systems that classify your thoughts without you deciding. That surface the right information without you searching. That nudge you toward the goals you’ve set without you having to remember them.

And you don’t have to be an engineer to build this. You just have to understand the patterns.

What this will feel like
Let me tell you what happens when this system is running.

You’ll feel lighter. Not because you’re more productive in some measurable way, but because you’re closing the open loops that live in your head. You’ll notice yourself thinking “I should remember that”—and instead of trying to hold it, you’ll post it to your inbox and move on. Your head will get clearer.

You’ll show up with more continuity for the people who matter to you. You’ll remember what they told you. You’ll follow up on things you promised. You’ll be the person who pays attention, because you have a system that pays attention for you.

Your projects will develop patterns you can see. Over time, you’ll notice what gets stuck, what moves smoothly, what you avoid. That visibility is the beginning of being able to change it.

Your anxiety—if you’re someone who tries to remember everything—won’t magically disappear. But it will change character. It stops being the background hum of all your untracked commitments and starts being a small set of next actions you can actually take.

It’s a factory for turning anxiety into action.

That’s the shift. From open loops to closed loops. From vague unease to concrete actions. From a brain that’s trying to store everything to a brain that’s free to think.

The invitation
I’m going to be honest with you.

Most people who read this won’t build the system. Not because it’s hard—it’s genuinely not. But because we’ve normalized the leakage that comes from being busy. We scroll through YouTube, we read another article, we tell ourselves we’ll get to it later. Later never comes.

Here’s what I want you to consider: the cost of a system you don’t build isn’t just missed ideas. It’s that your work becomes less compounding. The value you create in 2026 is lower because you’re not building on that value as intentionally as you could be.

If you’re thinking about what project might actually change your year, consider this one. You’re not building a productivity system to feel good about yourself. You’re giving your brain a support structure that helps it do what it was actually designed to do: think.

And in 2026, when AI multiplies whatever you bring to the table, that might be the highest-leverage investment you can make.

The guide is below. The principles are in your head now. The only question is whether you’ll build it.

GRAB THE GUIDE
This guide walks through every step: creating the Slack channel, setting up the five Notion databases, connecting Zapier, building the three automations, configuring the prompts. Total build time is 90 minutes if you’re comfortable with no-code tools, maybe two hours if you’re learning as you go.

The system is three Zapier automations, five Notion databases, and one Slack channel. The cost is roughly $20/month for Zapier plus a few dollars for API calls.

But here’s what I want you to understand: the guide is the tactical layer. What I’ve given you in this piece is the strategic layer—the principles that make the system work and that you can apply to anything else you build with AI.

When you understand why the bouncer exists, you’ll add confidence filters to every automation you build. When you understand why the receipt matters, you’ll log everything. When you understand why the fix button is essential, you’ll build easy correction mechanisms into every system.

That’s the real leverage. Not one second brain, but the ability to build infrastructure like this whenever you need it.

## User (18:46)

It's the second brain, but I'm also interested in the thinking like an engineer.

---

## Claude (18:46)



Right — the 12 principles aren't just about a second brain. They're a general engineering playbook for building anything reliable with AI. And you're already applying them to DET, to Chief, to your trading infrastructure.

Worth calling out the ones that should become standing rules for how we build:

**Principle 3: Prompts as APIs, not creative writing.** Fixed input, fixed output, no surprises. This is why memory_cli uses structured commands with positional args — not freeform "save this somewhere." When we build the CF automation (Phase 2 points system), the Playwright scraper should return strict JSON, not prose.

**Principle 5: Default safe when uncertain.** Don't guess, don't act, flag for review. This is why the KB bouncer concept matters — when the AI classifier isn't confident, hold the item instead of filing it wrong. We should apply this to any automation that touches real data.

**Principle 6: Small, frequent, actionable outputs.** Chief's morning digest should be <150 words. Three actions, one thing you're avoiding, one win. Not a 2,000-word report.

**Principle 12: Maintainability over cleverness.** Every tool we add is a failure point. When something breaks at 9 PM, you want a 5-minute fix, not an hour of debugging. This is why we keep Chief as a simple Python process, not a Kubernetes cluster.

**The meta-principle: separation of concerns.** Memory (DB/KB) is separate from compute (Atlas/Chief) is separate from interface (CLI/Slack/browser). You can swap any layer without rebuilding the others. That's why we can add Miro, add Discord pulls, add Schwab API — each one plugs in without breaking the core.

The "think like an engineer" mindset is really: **every system you build should be boring, predictable, and fixable by someone who didn't build it.** That includes future-you who forgot how it works.

Want me to distill these into a principles section for CLAUDE.md or a standalone doc? Could be a reference we check whenever we're building something new.

New deploy `121ef808` is queued from the fix commit. The manual one already errored (as expected — it had the old code). The new build should take about 3-5 minutes.

**What happened:** The Points code was all correct and tests passed, but the TS compiler in the Docker build is stricter than local dev — it treats unused interfaces as errors. ## Claude (19:07)

Honestly? Don't. Here's why:

1. **The video gave away the entire architecture.** We have the transcript — 7-state Gaussian HMM, 3 features (returns, range, volume change), Yahoo Finance data, 8 confirmation signals (RSI, momentum, volatility, volume, ADX, price, MACD). That's the whole thing.

2. **We have Claude Code.** The guy's whole pitch is "you need Claude Code to build this." We already have it. I can build the core HMM logic, the backtester, and the dashboard from what's in this transcript alone.

3. **$99 for copy-paste prompts** when you have an AI that can write the code directly from the concepts — that's paying for convenience you don't need.

4. **The real value isn't his code anyway.** It's tuning it to *our* thesis — Visser's turbulence model, credit spreads, scarcity/abundance framework. His Bitcoin-focused setup would need to be rebuilt for our use case regardless.

Save the $99. When we're ready to build it, I'll do it from the transcript. The hard part isn't the code — it's calibrating the regimes to our actual portfolio, which his community can't help with anyway.

## User (19:08)

So I guess you could find all the Python libraries necessary to do something like this, and then we got real-time data, probably historic data from Schwab. 

---

## Claude (19:08)

Exactly. We already have most of the stack:

**Data:**
- **Alpaca MCP** — real-time quotes, bars, snapshots (stocks, crypto, options)
- **Schwab API** — historical data, plus our actual positions
- **Yahoo Finance** (`yfinance`) — free hourly/daily candles, what the video uses

**Python libraries we'd need:**
- `hmmlearn` — the actual Gaussian HMM implementation (open source, well-maintained)
- `numpy`, `pandas`, `scikit-learn` — standard stuff, probably already installed
- `ta` or `ta-lib` — technical indicators (RSI, MACD, ADX)
- We already have `tools/data/technical_indicators.py` in the framework

**What we'd build:**
- Feed SPY, HYG, VIX, DXY (our thesis instruments) instead of just Bitcoin
- Train on daily candles (not hourly — we're not day-trading this)
- Output: "You're in regime X with Y% confidence" — bull, stress, crash, choppy, etc.
- Cross-reference with Visser's turbulence green bars as a second confirmation layer
- Simple dashboard, same pattern as the DET board

The Schwab historical data is the better source since it's our actual broker — no discrepancies. Alpaca fills in real-time. No new API keys needed, no new subscriptions.

When the time comes, it's probably a 2-day build. The math is a library call, not custom code. The real work is choosing the right features and tuning the regime labels to match our framework.

## User (19:12)

### Summary of Main Points from the Transcripts

You provided transcripts from two podcasts (with duplicates of each). I'll summarize the key takeaways from each, focusing on the content rather than the repetitions. These are forward-looking discussions: the Pomp podcast appears set in a futuristic 2025 scenario (Bitcoin at $118K, new regulations), while the Diamandis Moonshots episode discusses AI's long-term trajectory. I'll tie this to your preferences: companies for investment research, people/podcasts to follow, and trends in the financial environment.

#### 1. **Pomp Podcast: Bitcoin, Regulations, Retail Investors, and Athletes (Futuristic 2025 Context)**
   - **Bitcoin Update**: Bitcoin is stable at $118K. Trump Media bought $2B in Bitcoin, making it the 5th largest corporate holder. SEC Chair Paul Atkins supports public companies using Bitcoin/crypto as treasury assets.
   - **New Crypto Regulations ("Crypto Week")**: Three bills passed/signed: 
     - Genius Act: Federal framework for stablecoins (e.g., Tether, Circle) to boost US dollar dominance globally.
     - Clarity Act: Clarifies if digital assets are securities (SEC) or commodities (CFTC).
     - Anti-CBDC Act: Bans Federal Reserve from issuing a central bank digital currency due to privacy concerns.
     - Emphasis on stablecoins driving dollar adoption in emerging markets; banks (e.g., JP Morgan, Bank of America) will compete but challengers like Tether have a head start. Regulatory clarity favors disruptors over incumbents, leading to hyper-competition, M&A, and potential bank disruptions.
   - **Retail Investors Beating Wall Street**: Retail (self-directed investors) outperforms institutions by using online resources (X/Twitter, Reddit, podcasts). Examples: Retail led in Tesla, Palantir, Bitcoin. Discussion on Open Door stock (from 60¢ to $4+; analyst Eric Jackson predicts $82). GameStop rallied 24x in 5 years due to retail, now with $9.5B on balance sheet and Ryan Cohen as CEO.
   - **Athletes and Bitcoin**: Athletes like Saquon Barkley (converted $10M endorsements to Bitcoin in 2021, now ~$36M) and Russell Okung ("Russ") are pioneers, driven by generational wealth goals. More athletes adopting due to competitiveness and education; leagues warn against squandering earnings.
   - **Sponsors/Ads**: Polka Dot (blockchain tech), Core (Bitcoin yield), Bitcoin IRA (crypto in retirement).
   - **Overall Tone**: Bullish on Bitcoin/stablecoins as savings/spending tools; retail's edge in abundance era; global dollar strength via crypto.

#### 2. **Peter Diamandis Moonshots: Eric Schmidt on AI Superintelligence**
   - **AI Underhyped and Accelerating**: AI is a "learning machine" in network effects; growth limited by electricity, not chips. Massive energy demands: AI needs 92 GW more power in US (equivalent to 92 nuclear plants). Tech giants (Meta, Google, Microsoft, Amazon) signing nuclear deals (e.g., Meta with Constellation Energy).
   - **Timeline to Superintelligence**: Digital superintelligence (beyond human sum, like Einstein + da Vinci in your pocket) within 10 years. AGI-like capabilities (e.g., planning, deep memory) in 3-5 years; specialized AI "savants" in every field (math, programming, biology, physics) within 5 years. AI will replace junior programmers/math tasks soon; accelerate discoveries in materials, climate, biology.
   - **Energy and Hardware**: Nuclear (fission/fusion) won't scale fast enough; focus on efficiency (e.g., Nvidia Blackwell, AMD chips). Startups innovating in inference chips.
   - **Opportunities and Risks**: AI boosts productivity (30% YoY growth possible); enables abundance (e.g., new materials for decarbonization). Risks: Cyber/biological attacks, China race (they have electricity advantage; US chip bans may fail due to open-source/open-weights). National security concerns; proliferation (e.g., regulate models above 10^26 FLOPs). Startups' moats: Learning loops (fastest learner wins); patents in deep tech/hardware.
   - **Human Impact**: Not "Terminator" but "drift"—eroding agency/purpose if unregulated (Wall-E vs. Star Trek). AI won't eliminate jobs/purpose; enhances (e.g., lawyers with complex tools). Focus on human agency, aesthetics in AI era.
   - **Geopolitics/Business**: US vs. China; open-source may shift leadership to China. Enterprise AI (e.g., Google's GCP automates code/databases, disrupting 100K software firms). Consumer scale for massive growth.
   - **Overall Tone**: Optimistic on AI-driven abundance/productivity; urgent on energy, security, ethics. Eric Schmidt's book *Genesis* expands on this.

### 1. Companies Mentioned in a Positive Light (For Investment Research)
These are highlighted as innovative, disruptive, or performing well. Do your own due diligence—none are direct "buy" recommendations, but they're flagged for growth potential based on discussions.

- **From Pomp Podcast**:
  - **Bitcoin (as an asset)**: Strong store of value; corporate adoption (e.g., Trump Media's $2B buy). Athletes/professionals succeeding with it.
  - **Tether (USDT)**: Largest stablecoin; "winning" with massive users (400M+); profitable; FUD debunked; emerging market adoption.
  - **Circle (USDC)**: Growing stablecoin; regulatory clarity benefits.
  - **Polka Dot (DOT)**: Sponsor; scalable blockchain; compared to "AWS of Web3"; used by Mythical Games, Astro Network.
  - **Core (Bitcoin scaling)**: Sponsor; earn yield on Bitcoin without selling; DeFi ecosystem.
  - **Bitcoin IRA**: Sponsor; crypto in retirement accounts; trusted by 200K+ users.
  - **Open Door (ticker: OPNDF? – iBuying real estate)**: Potential 100x ("hundred bagger") per Eric Jackson; undervalued; meme stock rally but real trends.
  - **GameStop (GME)**: Up 24x in 5 years; $9.5B balance sheet; Ryan Cohen turning it around (e.g., Pokemon cards growth).
  - **Tesla (TSLA)**, **Palantir (PLTR)**: Retail-led successes; outperforming institutions.
  - **Banks (e.g., JP Morgan, Bank of America, Citi)**: Entering stablecoins with regulatory clarity; leveraging wealthy clients.

- **From Diamandis Moonshots**:
  - **Meta, Google (Alphabet), Microsoft, Amazon**: Leading AI/energy investments; e.g., nuclear deals, GCP for enterprise automation.
  - **Nvidia**: Chips powering AI (Blackwell); massive growth (stock up 1,500% in 3 years, profits up 1,425%).
  - **AMD**: Chip gains (e.g., 350 chip).
  - **Constellation Energy**: Nuclear partner for Meta (20-year deal).
  - **Startups in AI/hardware/energy**: Not named specifically, but Eric funds groups in physics models, inference chips, SMRs (small modular reactors), fusion.

**Research Notes**: Focus on AI/crypto/energy themes. Pomp emphasizes Bitcoin/stablecoins for treasury/wealth; Schmidt on AI giants for compute/power. Check valuations (e.g., Open Door volatile; Nvidia expensive but profit-backed).

### 2. Other Podcasts or People Mentioned to Follow
- **From Pomp Podcast**:
  - **People**: Eric Jackson (hedge fund manager; AI models for signals; follow on X for stock analysis). Ryan Cohen (GameStop CEO; turnaround expert). Saquon Barkley/Russell Okung (athletes; Bitcoin advocates). Wintermute CEO (recent episode on financial disruption).
  - **Podcasts**: Pomp's own (implied; daily emails/newsletters). CNBC appearances (e.g., Pomp's comments on politicians).

- **From Diamandis Moonshots**:
  - **People**: Eric Schmidt (ex-Google CEO; author of *Genesis*—read for AI/China insights). Peter Diamandis (host; abundance thesis; newsletter at diamandis.com/metatrends—covers AI, robotics, longevity). Dave London (co-host). Leopold Aschenbrenner (AI timeline to 2026-27). Ray Kurzweil (Singularity University; books on AI). Mark Andreessen (on AI risks/regulation). Dennis Hassabis (implied; AGI redefinitions).
  - **Podcasts**: Moonshots (Diamandis' show; follow for tech/AI guests). Facts vs. Feelings (mentioned indirectly via sentiment polls).

**Suggestions**: Follow Eric Schmidt/Peter Diamandis on X/LinkedIn for AI trends. Diamandis' newsletter (free; twice-weekly) aligns with your interest in meta-trends.

### 3. Trends and Changing Financial Environment
- **Crypto/Stablecoins Boom**: Regulatory clarity (e.g., Genius Act) accelerates adoption; stablecoins (Tether/Circle) challenge banks, boost USD globally. Bitcoin as savings asset; de-dollarization headlines false—people want dollars via digital rails. Friction reduction increases velocity to Bitcoin.
- **Retail vs. Institutions**: Rise of self-directed investors (via X/Reddit/podcasts); outperforming in Tesla/Palantir/Bitcoin. Abundance era: Sloshing capital, opportunities everywhere; focus on education/direct allocation.
- **AI Superintelligence**: Underhyped; 10-year timeline; energy bottleneck (nuclear deals). Productivity explosion (30% YoY growth); replaces programming/math tasks; biological/physics breakthroughs. Risks: China race, cyber/bio attacks, proliferation. Moats: Learning loops in startups. Human purpose: Enhances jobs, not eliminates; focus on agency/aesthetics.
- **Global Bull Market**: Strong earnings/margins drive stocks; Fed cuts without recession bullish. Hated rallies (fear gauges low) fuel upside. Broad participation (e.g., European financials, South Korea up 70%). Consumer pessimism skewed (politics/surveys); stimulus (e.g., Trump's $2K checks) could boost spending/assets.
- **Macro Shifts**: Mid-cycle slowdown (not recession); dovish Fed (rates to ~3%). Labor weakening but not cracking; inflation cooling (shelter down). Global revenue outpacing domestic for S&P firms.

Overall, both podcasts are bullish: Pomp on crypto/retail disruption; Schmidt on AI abundance. Trends point to tech/AI/crypto/energy as winners in a changing environment. If you attach more transcripts, I can analyze further!
